Divine Transcendence
beginner
5 min
God's Uniqueness: Difference by Essence Alone
Al-Iji cites the view of nafāt al-aḥwāl and Ash‘arī on divine transcendence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first theological view that God's essence is different from all other essences by virtue of itself alone, without any additional attribute or state.

Core Idea
God's essence is unique by itself, not by any added attribute.

Al-Iji begins the second objective by presenting the position of the negators of states (nafāt al-aḥwāl) and the doctrine of al-Ash'arī and Abū al-Ḥusayn al-Baṣrī. They held that the difference between any two existents is due to their essences alone, not any extra property. This means God shares no common reality with creation; any similarity is only in names and judgments. Consequently, God is absolutely transcendent above any peer (mithl) or rival (nidd).

Guided Path
1
Read the first view passage
Read the Arabic passage that presents the view of nafāt al-aḥwāl, al-Ash'arī, and Abū al-Ḥusayn al-Baṣrī. Notice the key phrase 'mukhālafa li-dhātihi al-makhṣūṣa lā li-amr zā'id'.
This is the core of the first view.
2
Identify the key terms
Identify the terms nafta al-aḥwāl, mukhālafa bi-al-dhāt, mithl, and nidd. Understand their meanings in context.
These terms are crucial for understanding the argument.
3
Reflect on transcendence
Consider why this view leads to the assertion that God is munazzah (transcendent) above mithl and nidd. What does it mean that there is no common quiddity?
This is a strong affirmation of tawḥīd.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المرصد الأول - المقصد الثاني : في أن ذاته تعالى مخالفة لسائر الذوات . إِليه ذهب نفاة الأحوال قالوا والمخالفة بينه وبينها لذاته المخصوصة لا لأمر زائد عليه وهو مذهب الشيخ الأشعري وأبي الحسين البصري فإنهما قالا المخالفة بين كل موجودين من الموجودات إنما هي بالذات، وليس بين الحقائق اشتراك إلا في الأسماء والأحكام دون الأجزاء المقومة، وعلى هذا فهو منزه عن المثل المشارك في تمام الماهية والند الذي هو المثل المنادي تعالى عن ذلك علواً كبيراً ، وقال قدماء المتكلمين ذاته تعالى مماثلة لسائر الذوات في الذاتية والحقيقة وإنما نمتاز عن سائر الذوات بأحوال أربعة الوجوب والحياة والعلم التام والقدرة التامة
The First Observatory – The Second Objective: Concerning that His essence, exalted is He, is different from other essences. To this went the negators of states (nafāt al-aḥwāl); they said: the difference between Him and them is for His specific essence, not for an additional matter. And this is the doctrine of al-Shaykh al-Ash‘arī and Abū al-Ḥusayn al-Baṣrī, for they both said: the difference between every two existents among existents is only by essence, and there is no commonality between realities except in names and judgments, not in the constitutive parts. On this, He is transcendent (munazzah) of any similar (mithl) sharing in the complete quiddity, and any rival (nidd) which is the opposing similar—exalted is He above that by great exaltation. And the early mutakallimun said: His essence is similar to other essences in essentiality and reality, and it is distinguished from other essences only by four states: necessity, life, complete knowledge, and complete power.
Source pages p. 17

Commentary
This passage introduces the second objective: that God's essence is different from all other essences. It presents two main views. The first view is that of the negators of states (nafāt al-aḥwāl) and of al-Ash‘arī and Abū al-Ḥusayn al-Baṣrī: difference between any two existents is by their essences alone, not by an additional attribute. Thus, God's essence is completely unique; He shares no common reality with any other being, only names and judgments. Therefore, He is transcendent above any similar in quiddity (mithl) or rival (nidd). The second view is that of the early mutakallimun: God's essence is similar to other essences in being a 'dhāt' (essence), but distinguished by four states (aḥwāl): wujūb (necessity), ḥayāh (life), ʿilm tāmm (complete knowledge), and qudra tāmma (complete power). The passage sets up a theological contrast between those who deny states and those who affirm them as distinguishing attributes.

Key Terms
نفاة الأحوال
مخالفة بالذات
مثل
ند

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to al-Ash'arī and Abū al-Ḥusayn al-Baṣrī, how is God's essence different from other essences?
A) By four additional states.
B) By His specific essence itself, not by anything additional.
C) By His knowledge and power.
D) By being free from all attributes.

Key Takeaway
God's essence is unique by itself, not by any added attribute; He transcends any similar or rival.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you say 'God is unique,' do you really accept that He is not like anything even in the most abstract sense, or do you still hold a mental image where He is a 'bigger' version of something you know? What false mental picture must you repent of today?
?
If God's essence is unique by itself and not by any added attribute, then He is worthy of worship solely for who He is—not for what He gives you. Does your prayer reflect that? Or are you only seeking His gifts? How will you change your worship starting now?
?
Since there is no common quiddity between God and creation, everything you see is utterly dependent and different from Him. How should this transform your awe of God and your attachment to created things? What one attachment will you cut today to honor His absolute uniqueness?

God's Uniqueness: Distinction by Four States
Al-Iji presents the early mutakallimun's view on divine uniqueness via four states.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the early mutakallimun's view that God's essence is similar to other essences in essentiality, but distinguished by four states: necessity, life, complete knowledge, and complete power.

Core Idea
God's essence is like others in concept but unique in four states.

After presenting the first view that God's essence is different by itself, Al-Iji now introduces the alternative position of the early mutakallimun (qadāmā al-mutakallimīn). They held that God's essence is similar to other essences in being a 'dhāt' (essence) and in the reality of essentiality, but it is distinguished by four states (aḥwāl) that are not essential parts of the quiddity. These states are wujūb (necessity), ḥayāh (life), ʿilm tāmm (complete knowledge), and qudra tāmma (complete power). This view allows for a degree of commonality while still affirming God's uniqueness.

Guided Path
1
Read the second view passage
Read the passage starting from 'wa qāla qadāmā al-mutakallimīn' to the end. Notice the contrast with the first view.
This view affirms similarity in essentiality.
2
List the four states
Identify the four states: wujūb, ḥayāh, ʿilm tāmm, and qudra tāmma. Understand why these are called 'states' (aḥwāl).
These are not essential attributes but distinguishing modalities.
3
Compare with the first view
Compare this view with the previous one. How does the understanding of similarity and difference shift? Which view is more inclusive of attributes?
The first view denies any shared quiddity; the second allows shared concept but adds states.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المرصد الأول - المقصد الثاني : في أن ذاته تعالى مخالفة لسائر الذوات . إِليه ذهب نفاة الأحوال قالوا والمخالفة بينه وبينها لذاته المخصوصة لا لأمر زائد عليه وهو مذهب الشيخ الأشعري وأبي الحسين البصري فإنهما قالا المخالفة بين كل موجودين من الموجودات إنما هي بالذات، وليس بين الحقائق اشتراك إلا في الأسماء والأحكام دون الأجزاء المقومة، وعلى هذا فهو منزه عن المثل المشارك في تمام الماهية والند الذي هو المثل المنادي تعالى عن ذلك علواً كبيراً ، وقال قدماء المتكلمين ذاته تعالى مماثلة لسائر الذوات في الذاتية والحقيقة وإنما نمتاز عن سائر الذوات بأحوال أربعة الوجوب والحياة والعلم التام والقدرة التامة
The First Observatory – The Second Objective: Concerning that His essence, exalted is He, is different from other essences. To this went the negators of states (nafāt al-aḥwāl); they said: the difference between Him and them is for His specific essence, not for an additional matter. And this is the doctrine of al-Shaykh al-Ash‘arī and Abū al-Ḥusayn al-Baṣrī, for they both said: the difference between every two existents among existents is only by essence, and there is no commonality between realities except in names and judgments, not in the constitutive parts. On this, He is transcendent (munazzah) of any similar (mithl) sharing in the complete quiddity, and any rival (nidd) which is the opposing similar—exalted is He above that by great exaltation. And the early mutakallimun said: His essence is similar to other essences in essentiality and reality, and it is distinguished from other essences only by four states: necessity, life, complete knowledge, and complete power.
Source pages p. 17

Commentary
This passage introduces the second objective: that God's essence is different from all other essences. It presents two main views. The first view is that of the negators of states (nafāt al-aḥwāl) and of al-Ash‘arī and Abū al-Ḥusayn al-Baṣrī: difference between any two existents is by their essences alone, not by an additional attribute. Thus, God's essence is completely unique; He shares no common reality with any other being, only names and judgments. Therefore, He is transcendent above any similar in quiddity (mithl) or rival (nidd). The second view is that of the early mutakallimun: God's essence is similar to other essences in being a 'dhāt' (essence), but distinguished by four states (aḥwāl): wujūb (necessity), ḥayāh (life), ʿilm tāmm (complete knowledge), and qudra tāmma (complete power). The passage sets up a theological contrast between those who deny states and those who affirm them as distinguishing attributes.

Key Terms

الوجوب
الحياة
العلم التام
القدرة التامة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that 'similarity in essentiality' means God shares a genus or species with created beings.
Correction: The passage means only the concept of 'essence' is similar, not that there is a common real quiddity. The distinction by states ensures God is unique.

Checkpoint
According to the early mutakallimun, what distinguishes God's essence from other essences?
A) His essence is different by itself.
B) Four additional states: necessity, life, complete knowledge, complete power.
C) The negation of all attributes.
D) The absence of any similarity.

Key Takeaway
The early mutakallimun affirmed a conceptual similarity between God's essence and others, but with unique distinguishing states.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you reflect on the four states that distinguish God – necessity, life, complete knowledge, complete power – which one do you most often take for granted? How does your life change if you truly live with the awareness that God has complete knowledge of every thought and action? What will you do today to remind yourself of this truth?
?
The early mutakallimun said God's essence is similar in concept to other essences. Does this make God feel more relatable or more distant to you? Honestly examine your heart: do you secretly desire a God who is entirely different, or one who is intimately like you? How does this lesson challenge your assumption?
?
If God's uniqueness is defined by these four states, and you lack any of them in perfection, what does that mean for your worship? Is your worship based on the recognition of His necessity, life, knowledge, and power? Give one concrete example of how you can deepen your reverence for God's complete power this week.

Divine Transcendence

The Ash'ari View on Distinction by Essence Alone
Explains the Ash'ari position that difference between existents is by essence alone.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Ash'ari doctrine that the difference between existents is by essence alone, not by an added attribute or state, and that God is transcendent above any similarity.

Core Idea
Distinction is by essence, not by anything added.

This passage presents the core Ash'ari doctrine on how created beings differ from each other and from God. It clarifies that the difference is not due to any extra attribute but to the very nature of each essence. This principle safeguards God's uniqueness (tawhid) and refutes those who posit common realities (haqiqah mushtarakah) across diverse existents.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice the technical terms like 'نفاة الأحوال' and 'المخالفة'.
2
Identify the main claims
Find the claim about the negators of states, the general principle applied by al-Ash'ari and al-Basri, and the conclusion about God's transcendence above mithl and nidd.
3
Understand the nuance of 'no commonality'
Note that commonality is accepted in names and judgments, not in realities. This is important to avoid the misunderstanding that al-Ash'ari denies any commonality at all.
This distinguishes the Ash'ari view from pure nominalism.
4
Reflect on the significance
Consider how this doctrine protects divine uniqueness and how it differs from other theological schools, such as those that posit states (ahwal).
This passage is key to understanding Ash'ari theology of tawhid.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
إليه ذهب نفاة الأحوال قالوا والمخالفة بينه وبينها لذاته المخصوصة لا لأمر زائد عليه وهو مذهب الشيخ الأشعري وأبي الحسين البصري فإنهما قالا المخالفة بين كل موجودين من الموجودات إنما هي بالذات، وليس بين الحقائق اشتراك إلا في الأسماء والأحكام دون الأجزاء المقومة، وعلى هذا فهو منزه عن المثل المشارك في تمام الماهية ) والند ) الذي هو المثل المنادي ) تعالى عن ذلك علواً كبيراً . قوله : ( فإنهما قالا المخالفة بين كل موجودين أي بين كل شخصين موجودين فليس بين أفراد الإنسان عندهما حقيقة مشتركة، وهذا هو المستفاد من ظاهر قول الشيخ أن وجود كل نفيه الوجود المطلق، وإن أوله المصنف فيما سبق بأن معناه أنه ليس شيء عين حقيقته للوجود هوية مغايرة لهوية الموجود . قوله : (فهو منزه عن المثل) إما في ذاته فلما ذكره وإما في صفاته فلأن شيئاً من الممكنات لا يسد مسده في شيء من الصفات، وكأن ذكر الند بعد المثل يشير إلى الثاني . قوله هو المثل (المنادي) أي المعادي وأصله الهمز لأنه من الندء وهو النهوض، وكأن المعاديين ينهض كل منهما إلى الآخر.
The negators of states went to this: they said the difference between Him and them is due to His specific essence, not due to something additional to it. This is the school of Shaykh al-Ash'ari and Abu al-Husayn al-Basri, for they both said: the difference between every two existents among existents is only by essence, and there is no commonality between realities except in names and judgments, not in the constituent parts. On this basis, He is transcendent above the similar that shares in complete quiddity, and the opponent (al-nidd) which is the similar that opposes (al-munadi) — exalted is He above that greatly. His saying: 'for they both said the difference between every two existents' means between every two existing persons, so according to them there is no common reality between individuals of man. This is what is understood from the apparent meaning of the Shaykh's statement that the existence of each negates absolute existence, although the author previously interpreted it as meaning that nothing is the very essence of its reality, for existence has an identity different from the identity of the existent. His saying: 'He is transcendent above the similar' either in His essence due to what he mentioned, or in His attributes because no possible thing can take its place in any of the attributes, and it seems that mentioning the opponent (nidd) after the similar indicates the second. His saying: 'the similar (al-munadi)' meaning the adversary, originally it is with hamz because it is from nida' meaning rising, and as if the adversaries each rise against the other.
Source pages p. 17

Commentary
This passage defines the position of the negators of states (nufat al-ahwal) regarding God's distinction from other beings. They assert that the difference (mukhalafah) between God and other essences is due to His specific essence (dhat makhsusah) itself, not due to any additional attribute. This is also the school of al-Ash'ari and Abu al-Husayn al-Basri, who extended this principle to all existents: any two existents differ by their essences, and there is no commonality in reality (haqiqah) except in names and judgments (asma' wa ahkam), not in the constitutive parts (ajza' muqawwimah). Consequently, God is free from any similar (mithl) that shares in complete quiddity (tamam al-mahiyyah) and from any opponent (nidd), which is a similar that opposes. The commentary then explains each phrase: 'between every two existents' means between any two existing persons, so for them there is no common reality (haqiqah mushtarakah) between individuals of a species like humans. The apparent meaning of al-Ash'ari's statement that 'the existence of each negates absolute existence' is interpreted as meaning that existence has an identity distinct from the identity of the existent. Then it explains 'transcendent above the similar' either in essence (as mentioned) or in attributes, because nothing possible can substitute for Him in any attribute. The mention of 'nidd' after 'mithl' suggests the second meaning (attributes). Finally, 'al-munadi' is derived from nida' (rising), meaning the adversary who rises against another.

Key Terms
نفاة الأحوال
المثل
الند
الذات
المخالفة
الأسماء والأحكام
الأجزاء المقومة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That al-Ash'ari denies any commonality at all between existents.
Correction: Al-Ash'ari accepts commonality in names and judgments (asma' wa ahkam), but not in realities (haqa'iq) or constitutive parts (ajza' muqawwimah).

Checkpoint
According to the negators of states, what is the basis of distinction between God and other beings?
A) An additional attribute
B) The essence itself
C) A state
D) Accidents

Key Takeaway
The Ash'ari position holds that all existents differ by their essences alone, and God is free from any similarity or opposition.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you truly believe that nothing shares in God's essence, why do you sometimes hope that something other than God can fulfill you? How can you realign your heart to see only Him as the source of all?
?
The Ash'ari says difference is by essence alone. In your life, do you treat God as essentially different or do you reduce Him to a human-like figure? What steps can you take to deepen your awe of His transcendence?
?
You claim God is unique, but do you ever act as if there is a 'nidd' in your life—something or someone you oppose God with? How can you uproot that hidden shirk from your heart?

Divine Transcendence
beginner

The First Purpose: God is Not in a Direction or Place
The author states the first purpose of the second observatory on transcendence, negating direction and place.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Islamic theology affirms that Allah is not in any direction or place, and how this differs from anthropomorphist views that assign Him a physical location.

Core Idea
True transcendence begins with negating physical location from Allah.

This passage opens the second observatory on God's transcendence, which deals with negative attributes. The first purpose is to establish that God is not in any direction or place, contrary to anthropomorphists who assign Him a physical location. The commentator also explains why negative attributes are given priority.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and its translation
Read the Arabic text once, then the English translation. Notice the structure: the author states a principle, then lists opposing views.
The passage includes both the matn (text) and a commentary (sharh).
2
Identify the three key claims
Find where the author negates direction, negates place, and then presents the anthropomorphist disagreement. Mark these three segments.
The negation is clear in the first sentence of the purpose.
3
Understand the anthropomorphist views
List the different opinions among the anthropomorphists: Ibn Karram's view (contact with Throne, motion), the Jews' view (groaning, surplus fingers), the extreme claims (embrace), and the finite/infinite distance views.
These views all share the idea that God is in a direction (above).
4
Reflect on the commentator's clarification
Read the commentator's remarks on why negative attributes are called attributes of majesty and why they are given precedence. Also note his definitions of direction and place to avoid confusion.
The commentator emphasizes that transcendence (tanzih) is the main purpose of prophethood.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المرصد الثاني في تنزيهه ]
( وهي الصفات السلبية وفيه مقاصد ) سبعة .
المقصد الأول : أنه تعالى ليس في جهة ولا في مكان ]
( أنه تعالى ليس في جهة من الجهات ولا) في مكان من الأمكنة ( وخالف فيه ) ( المشبهة وخصصوه بجهة الفوق ( اتفاقا ( ثم اختلفوا فيما بينهم، ( فذهب ) أبو عبد الله ( محمد بن كرام إلى أن كونه في الجهة ككون الأجسام فيها ) وهو أن يكون بحيث يشار إليه أنه هاهنا أو هناك قال ( وهو مماس للصفحة العيا من العرش ويجوز عليه الحركة والانتقال وتبدل الجهات وعليه اليهود حتى قالوا العرش يئط من تحته أطيط الرحل الجديد ( تحت الركب الثقيل ( و ) قالوا : إنه يفضل على العرش من كل جهة أربعة أصابع وزاد بعض المشبهة كمضر وكهمس وأحمد الهجيمي أن المخلصين) من المؤمنين ( يعانقونه في الدنيا والآخرة ومنهم من قال ) هو ( محاذ للعرش غير مماس ،له فقيل ) بعده عنه ( بمسافة متناهية وقيل : ) بمسافة (غير متناهية،
قوله : ( وهي الصفات السلبية وتسمى بصفات الجلال. كما أن الوجودية تسمى بصفات الإكرام وإنما قدم الصفات السلبية على الوجودية إهتماماً بشأنها فإن التنزيه عن النقائص أهم من إثبات صفات وجودية زائدة على الذات وهذا التنزيه هو معظم المقصود من بعثة الأنبياء عليهم السلام كما أشرنا إليه في أوائل الكتاب . قوله: (ليس في جهة ولا في (مكان إن أريد بالجهة منتهى الإشارات الحسية أو الحركات المستقيمة تكون الجهات أطرافاً وحدوداً للأمكنة لأنها حينئذ تكون عبارة عن نهاية البعد الذي المكان، وإن أريد بها المكان الذي هي جهة ما تكون عبارة عن نفس المكان باعتبار إضافة ما ثم الملازمة بين نفي المكان ونفي الجهة إن سلمت لم يقدح في الجمع بين النفيين، لأن المقام مقام التفصيل والتوضيح كما لا يخفى . قوله : ( إلي أن كونه في الجهة إلخ) أي في جهة الفوق كما هو الظاهر من قوله وخصصوه بجهة الفوق ،اتفاقا وكذا المراد من الجهة في قوله ومنهم من قال : ليس كونه في الجهة إلخ جهة هو الفوق . قوله : ( أطيط الرح) الأطيط صوت الرحل والإبل من ثقل أحمالها، وكذا صوت الجوف من الخوى .
The Second Observatory on His Transcendence. And they are the negative attributes, and it contains seven purposes. The First Purpose: That He, exalted, is not in a direction nor in a place. That He, exalted, is not in any direction nor in any place. And the anthropomorphists (al-mushabbiha) disagreed with this and specified Him with the direction of above, unanimously. Then they differed among themselves. Abu Abdullah Muhammad ibn Karram went to that His being in a direction is like the being of bodies in it, i.e., that He is such that one can point to Him as being here or there. He said: He is in contact with the upper surface of the Throne, and motion, transference, and change of directions are permissible for Him. The Jews also hold this, so much so that they said the Throne groans beneath Him like the groaning of a new saddle under a heavy load, and they said: He has a surplus of four fingers over the Throne from every side. And some anthropomorphists like Mudar, Kahmas, and Ahmad al-Hujaymi added that the sincere ones among the believers embrace Him in this world and the Hereafter. And among them is one who said: He is opposite the Throne, not in contact with it. Then it was said: He is at a finite distance from it, and it was said: at an infinite distance. His saying: (And they are the negative attributes and are called the attributes of majesty, just as the positive attributes are called the attributes of generosity. The negative attributes were given precedence over the positive attributes out of concern for their status, for transcendence above imperfections is more important than affirming positive attributes additional to the essence, and this transcendence is the main objective of the mission of the prophets, peace be upon them, as we have indicated at the beginning of the book.) His saying: (Not in a direction nor in a place) if by direction is meant the limit of sensory indications or straight movements, then directions are boundaries and limits of places because they would then be expressions for the end of the dimension that is the place; and if by it is meant the place which is a certain direction, then it is an expression for the place itself with consideration of some relation. Then the necessary correlation between the negation of place and the negation of direction, if granted, does not harm the combination of the two negations, because the context is one of detail and clarification, as is not hidden. His saying: (that His being in a direction etc.) meaning in the direction of above, as is apparent from his saying 'and they specified Him with the direction of above, unanimously.' Likewise, what is meant by direction in his saying 'and among them is one who said: His being in a direction etc.' is the direction of above. His saying: (the groaning of the saddle) 'al-atit' is the sound of the saddle and camels from the weight of their loads, and likewise the sound of the belly from emptiness.
Source pages p. 22

Commentary
This passage begins the second observatory on God's transcendence, which deals with negative attributes. The author first states that this section contains seven purposes, the first being that God is not in any direction or place. He then notes that the anthropomorphists (al-mushabbiha) disagreed and unanimously assigned Him the direction of above, though they differed among themselves. The commentator details the extreme views: Ibn Karram held that God's being in a direction is like that of bodies—He touches the Throne, can move, etc. The Jews similarly said the Throne groans under Him and that He exceeds it by four fingers. Some extreme anthropomorphists even claimed that the faithful embrace God in this world and the Hereafter. Others said He is opposite the Throne without contact, at either a finite or infinite distance. Then the commentator explains why the negative attributes are called attributes of majesty and are given precedence: transcendence above imperfections is more important than affirming positive attributes, and this is the main purpose of the prophets' mission. He then clarifies the terms 'direction' and 'place': if direction means the limit of sensory indication, it is part of place; if it means place itself, then each implies the other. Therefore, negating both together is not contradictory, as the context demands thorough clarification. Finally, he glosses 'al-atit' as the sound of a saddle or animal groan from heaviness.

Key Terms

مكان
تنزيه
تشبيه
مشبهة
صفات سلبية
صفات جلال

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that negating direction and place from Allah implies He is non-existent or limited.
Correction: The passage clarifies that negation of physical attributes is the foundation of tanzih, affirming Allah's absolute transcendence without corporeality.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which group unanimously affirmed that God is in the direction of above?
A) The Mu'tazila
B) The Ash'aris
C) The anthropomorphists (al-mushabbiha)
D) The Jews

Key Takeaway
The first purpose of transcendence is to negate direction and place from Allah, which is the foundation of tawhid.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you ever catch yourself imagining Allah as being 'up there' in a physical sense? What concrete step can you take today to replace that image with a purely transcendent understanding of His existence?
?
The author says tanzih is the main objective of prophethood. If you truly stripped away every mental picture of Allah having a location, how would that change the intensity and focus of your worship?
?
When you feel distant from Allah, do you subconsciously think of Him as 'far away' in space? How does this lesson challenge you to redefine nearness to Allah without any reference to place or direction?

Divine Transcendence
beginner
5 min
God is Not in Direction or Place: The Verbal Dispute
Opening statement and identification of verbal dispute

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the foundational position that God transcends direction and place, and why the dispute with those who affirm direction only for God is verbal, not semantic.

Core Idea
God is above all directions — the disagreement is just about words.

This lesson opens the discussion on divine transcendence in the second observatory. The author states unequivocally that God is not in a direction (jiha) nor in a place (makan). He immediately addresses a dissenting view that tries to reconcile the idea of direction by saying God is in a direction but not like bodies. The author clarifies that the dispute with such a person is merely verbal, not about real meaning, and that such verbal usage depends on scriptural revelation.

Guided Path
1
Read the opening declaration
Read the first sentence carefully: 'He is not in a direction nor in a place.' This is a clear negation. Notice the absolute language.
2
Understand the dissenting view
The second sentence introduces someone who says God is in a direction but unlike bodies. Consider why someone might think this way.
3
Grasp the response
The author says the dispute is verbal (lafẓī), not semantic (ma‘nawī). What does that mean? It means they agree on the meaning but disagree on the label.
4
Reflect on the role of revelation
The phrase 'verbal usage depends on the arrival of the Law' means that such terms can only be used if scripture permits. This shows the importance of textual evidence in kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المرصد الثاني - المقصد الأول : أنه تعالى ليس في جهة ولا في مكان
ومنهم من قال : ليس كونه في الجهة ككون الأجسام في الجهة والمنازعة مع هذا القائل راجعة إلى اللفظ دون المعنى والاطلاق اللفظي متوقف على ورود الشرع به
لنا في إثبات هذا المطلوب وجوه الأول لو كان الرب تعالى في مكان أو جهة لزم قدم المكان أو الجهة وقد برهنا أن لا قديم سوى الله تعالى وعليه الاتفاق من المتخاصمين.
قوله : ( راجعة إلى اللفظ دون المعنى قيل فيه بحث لأن إثبات الجهة لا كجهات الأجسام ليس كنفيها، كما أن إثبات الرؤية لا كرؤية الأجسام ليس كنفيها، وأجيب بأن الرؤية المذكورة لها معنى يصلح محلا للنزاع، وهو الانكشاف التام البصري بلا جهة ومقابلة بخلاف الجهة فإن المنفي منها هو الجهة المشار إليها بهاهنا أو هناك وأما ما سواها فمسكوت عنه لا منفي .
قوله : (لزم قدم (المكان إن أراد به إلزام المشبهة القائلين بكونه تعالى متمكناً على العرمش صح اللزوم لكن لا يكون دليلا على مطلق انتفاء المكان وإن أراد به البرهان على هذا المطلب العالي ورد عليه أنه إنما يتم إذا قلنا بأن المكان هو السطح أو البعد المجرد الموجود، وأما إذا قلنا بأنه الخلاء المتوهم كما هو المذهب فلا لأن القديم موجود لا أوله ولا ولا وجود للمكان حينئذ ، اللهم إلا أن يدعي أن الفراغ محاط بشيء البتة فيلزم قدم المحيط وفيه منع ظاهر وإن أراد بالقدم الأزلية ورد عليه أن المبرهن عليه فيما سبق نفي القدم بالمعنى الأول عما سوى ذاته تعالى لا نفي الأزلية كيف وإعدام الحوادث أزلية بلا مرية .
The second observatory - the first purpose: That He, exalted, is not in a direction nor in a place. And among them is he who said: His being in a direction is not like the being of bodies in a direction; and the dispute with this speaker is verbal, not semantic, and verbal usage depends on the arrival of the Law. We have for proving this desired [point] proofs. The first: If the Lord, exalted, were in a place or direction, the eternity of the place or direction would follow, and we have proven that there is no eternal other than God, exalted, and upon this there is agreement among the disputants. His statement: 'It is verbal, not semantic' — it was said: There is a discussion because affirming a direction not like the directions of bodies is not like negating it, just as affirming vision not like the vision of bodies is not like negating it. And it was answered that the mentioned vision has a meaning that is suitable as a locus of dispute, which is complete ocular disclosure without direction or opposition, unlike direction, for the negated from it is the direction indicated by 'here' or 'there', and as for other than that, it is passed over in silence, not negated. His statement: 'The eternity of the place would follow' — if he intends by it to obligate the anthropomorphists who say that He, exalted, is firmly established on the Throne, then the implication is correct, but it is not a proof for the absolute negation of place. And if he intends by it a proof for this lofty matter, it is objected that it is only complete if we say that place is the surface or the abstract dimension that exists. But if we say that it is the imagined void, as is the doctrine, then no, because the eternal exists without beginning and there is no existence of place then. Unless one claims that the void is surrounded by something definitively, then the eternity of the surrounding would follow, and there is an apparent refutation. And if he intends by 'eternity' beginninglessness, it is objected that what was proven before is the negation of eternity in the first sense from other than His essence, not the negation of beginninglessness; how? while the nonexistences of accidents are beginningless without doubt.
Source pages p. 23

Commentary
This passage opens the second observatory, first purpose, with the definitive statement that God is not in a direction (jiha) nor in a place (makan). It immediately addresses a dissenting view: someone says that God is in a direction but not like bodies. The author responds that the dispute with this person is merely verbal (lafẓī), not semantic (ma‘nawī), and such verbal usage depends on scriptural revelation. Then the author presents the first proof from multiple proofs: If God were in a place or direction, that place or direction would be eternal (qadīm), but we have proven that nothing is eternal except God, and all parties agree. The commentator then explains the remark about the dispute being verbal: an objection is raised that affirming a direction unlike bodies' directions is not the same as negating it, analogously to the case of vision (ru’ya). The answer is that vision has a real meaning (complete ocular disclosure without direction or opposition) that can be debated, unlike direction, where what is negated is only specific indication. The commentator then analyzes the proof about eternity: if meant to obligate anthropomorphists, it works but doesn't prove absolute negation; if meant as a proof, it depends on the definition of place (surface vs. imagined void). If place is the imagined void, then no eternity follows because the eternal exists without it. Also, if 'eternity' means beginninglessness (azaliyya), the proof fails because only eternity in the sense of pre-eternity of existence is negated for others, not beginninglessness—since nonexistences are beginningless.

Key Terms
جهة
مكان
لفظ
معنى

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the author's immediate response to the person who says God is in a direction unlike bodies?
A) He says it is a semantic dispute
B) He says it is a verbal dispute
C) He says it is a fundamental disagreement
D) He says it is an error

Key Takeaway
The debate over whether God is in a direction is not about reality but about the permissibility of using certain words.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you describe God, do you ever use words that have no clear scriptural basis, simply because they feel right? How might this habit lead you into a verbal dispute that distracts from the truth?
?
Think of a recent debate you had about religion. Was it truly about meaning, or did it become a quarrel over words? What would it cost you to drop the verbal battle and focus on the agreed core?

Divine Transcendence

6 min
The First Proof: Eternity of Place or Direction
First proof from eternity and clarification of verbal dispute

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first proof that if God were in a place or direction, that place or direction would be eternal, which contradicts the principle that only God is eternal.

Core Idea
If God were in a place, that place would be eternal — but nothing is eternal but God.

After establishing the topic, the author presents the first proof for God's transcendence of place and direction. The proof is a conditional argument: if God were in a place or direction, that place/direction would be eternal. But it is agreed that nothing other than God is eternal. Therefore, God cannot be in a place or direction. The passage also includes a clarifying discussion about why the earlier verbal dispute is not semantic.

Guided Path
1
Read the proof
Read the conditional statement: 'If the Lord were in a place or direction, the eternity of the place or direction would follow.' Notice the logical structure.
2
Understand the premise
The author says 'we have proven that there is no eternal other than God, and upon this there is agreement.' This is a key premise accepted by all sides.
3
Examine the objection about vision
Read the objection that affirming a direction unlike bodies' directions is not the same as negating it, using the analogy of vision. Understand the counter-response.
The response clarifies that vision has a real meaning (disclosure without direction) while direction does not.
4
Grasp the conclusion
The overall argument concludes that God cannot be in a place or direction because that would necessitate an eternal besides God.

Source Text

?

Analyzing the Proof: Definitions of Place and Eternity
Critical analysis of first proof: definitions of place and eternity

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the first proof is analyzed, specifically how different definitions of 'place' (surface vs. imagined void) and 'eternity' (qidam vs. azaliyya) affect its validity.

Core Idea
The proof's strength depends on how you define 'place' and 'eternity'.

This lesson delves into the detailed analysis of the first proof. The commentator examines the proof by considering different meanings of 'place' (makan): if place is a surface or abstract dimension, the proof works; but if place is the imagined void (al-khala' al-mutawahham), then the proof fails because the eternal exists without a place. Additionally, the commentator distinguishes between two senses of 'eternity': qidam (pre-eternity of existence) and azaliyya (beginninglessness). The proof only negates qidam for other things, not azaliyya, since nonexistences (adam) are beginningless.

Guided Path
1
Read the analysis of 'place'
The passage considers two definitions of place: (1) surface or abstract dimension, and (2) imagined void. Understand why the proof only works under the first definition.
2
Consider the imagined void
If place is the imagined void, the proof fails because the eternal exists without a beginning and there is no place at that time. The objection about the void being surrounded is also refuted.
3
Distinguish two meanings of 'eternity'
If 'eternity' (qidam) means beginninglessness (azaliyya), then the proof's premise (only God is eternal) is false because nonexistences are beginningless. The author clarifies that the previous proof negated qidam in the sense of pre-eternity of existence, not azaliyya.

The commentator then explains the remark about the dispute being verbal: an objection is raised that affirming a direction unlike bodies' directions is not the same as negating it, analogously to the case of vision (ru’ya). The answer is that vision has a real meaning (complete ocular disclosure without direction or opposition) that can be debated, unlike direction, where what is negated is only specific indication. The commentator then analyzes the proof about eternity: if meant to obligate anthropomorphists, it works but doesn't prove absolute negation; if meant as a proof, it depends on the definition of place (surface vs. imagined void). If place is the imagined void, then no eternity follows because the eternal exists without it. Also, if 'eternity' means beginninglessness (azaliyya), the proof fails because only eternity in the sense of pre-eternity of existence is negated for others, not beginninglessness—since nonexistences are beginningless.

Key Terms
قدم
أزلية
مكان
خلاء
سطح
بعد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The first proof is universally valid regardless of definitions.
Correction: The proof depends on definitions of 'place' (surface vs. void) and 'eternity' (qidam vs. azaliyya).

Checkpoint
According to the analysis, if 'place' is defined as the imagined void, what happens to the first proof?
A) The proof becomes stronger
B) The proof fails
C) The proof is unaffected
D) The proof becomes invalid only if 'eternity' means beginninglessness

Key Takeaway
The validity of the first proof hinges on accepting specific definitions of place and eternity, and it does not hold under all interpretations.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You just examined a proof that many scholars relied on. Are you willing to hold your own beliefs about God to the same standard of logical scrutiny, even if it means discovering that some of your proofs are weaker than you thought? What will you do with that discomfort?
?
The validity of the proof hinges on definitions you may never have considered. How many of your theological convictions are based on assumptions you have not defined? Will you take time this week to identify one key term in your faith that you assume everyone means the same way, and then check if that is true?
?
The author shows that a proof can be valid for one purpose but not another. Could it be that you have been using a proof for a purpose it was never meant to serve? If so, what would you need to change in how you defend your beliefs?

Second Proof: Need for Place Implies Possibility
Second proof: dependency of occupant on place leads to absurdities.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second proof against God being in a place: if He were an occupant of place, He would depend on it, leading to the absurd conclusions of His possibility and the place's necessity.

Core Idea
Dependence on place contradicts God's absolute self-sufficiency.

The author now presents a second argument against the notion that God occupies a place. This proof focuses on the relationship between an occupant and its place: the occupant needs the place, but the place does not need the occupant. This asymmetry forces two impossible conclusions if applied to God. Let's examine the reasoning and the objections that follow.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Note the structure: the main proof, then two consequences, each addressed separately, and finally the correct argument.
2
Understand the asymmetry
Identify why the occupant depends on place while place is independent of occupant. The key concept is the permissibility of void (jawāz al-khalā’).
3
Examine the first false implication
The proof claims that if God were an occupant, He would become possible (imkān). See how the commentator defends this implication against an objection.
The objection analogizes God's need for place with His need for a created thing as a creator.
4
Examine the second false implication
The proof also claims that the place would become necessary (wujūb). Understand why this is problematic even if place exists.
The commentator points out this assumes place exists, which is not the view of the mutakallimūn.
5
Learn the correct argument
The commentator offers a sound proof: if God were in space, He would be subject to spatial modes (akwān), which are accidents, leading to His temporality—an impossibility.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثاني المتمكن محتاج إلى مكانه بحيث يستحيل وجوده بدونه، والمكان مستغن عن المتمكن لجواز الخلاء فيلزم إمكان الواجب ووجوب المكان وكلاهما باطل.
قوله : ( فيلزم إمكان الواجب فإن قلت إنما يلزم إمكان الواجب إذا لزم الاحتياج إلى الغير في الوجود واللازم مما ذكر هو الاحتياج إليه في الوجود مالاً ولا شك أنه ينافي الوجوب الذاتي الذي هو منشأ الاستغناء المطلق، فمنع لزوم إمكان الواجب قياساً على الاحتياج في كونه خالقاً إلى ما صدر عنه غلط .
قوله : ( ووجوب المكان فيه أنه أيضاً مبني على وجود المكان فلا يستقيم على رأي المتكلمين وعلى تقدير وجوده لا يتم أيضاً، لأن اللازم من جواز الخلاء عدم احتياج المكان إلى كون المتمكن شاغلاً فيه، وهذا لا يستلزم استغناؤه عنه في وجوده فالصواب أن يستدل على هذا المطلب بأنه لو كان الواجب تعالى متحيزاً لم يكن منفكاً عن الأكوان ضرورة، فيلزم حدوثه لأن الأكوان موجودة عند المتكلمين أيضاً كما سبق، وكل موجود سوى ذاته تعالى حادث فيكون الباري تعالى حينئذ محلاً للحوادث وما لا يخلو عن الحوادث فهو حادث .
The second: The occupant (al-mutamakkin) is in need of its place, such that its existence is impossible without it, and the place is independent of the occupant due to the permissibility of void, so the possibility (imkān) of the Necessary (al-wājib) and the necessity (wujūb) of the place follow, and both are false. His statement: 'So the possibility of the Necessary follows' — for if you say, the possibility of the Necessary only follows if need for another in existence follows, and what follows from what is mentioned is need for it in existence, and there is no doubt that it negates the essential necessity (al-wujūb al-dhātī) which is the source of absolute independence, so denying the implication of possibility of the Necessary by analogy with the need, in His being a creator, for that which emanates from Him is an error. His statement: 'and the necessity of the place' — in it, that it is also based on the existence of place, so it is not sound according to the view of the mutakallimūn, and on the assumption of its existence it is also not complete, because what follows from the permissibility of void is the lack of need of the place for the occupant to occupy it, and this does not necessitate its independence from it in its existence. So the correct thing is to argue for this matter: If the Necessary, exalted, were located in space (mutahayyiz), it would not be free from spatial modes (akwān) necessarily, so its temporality (ḥudūth) would follow, because spatial modes exist according to the mutakallimūn as well, as previously mentioned, and every existent other than His essence, exalted, is temporal, so then the Creator, exalted, would be a locus for accidents, and whatever is not free from accidents is temporal.
Source pages p. 23

Commentary
This passage is the second proof (al-thānī) from the author's list. It proceeds as follows: The occupant (al-mutamakkin) — that which exists in a place — is necessarily dependent on its place for existence. The place, however, is independent of the occupant because void (khalā’) is conceivable (jā’iz al-khalā'). This asymmetry forces two conclusions: first, the Necessary (al-wājib) would become possible (imkān) because it would depend on another; second, the place itself would be necessary (wajaba al-makān). Both are absurd. The commentator then examines each conclusion. Regarding the first (imkān al-wājib), an objection is raised: possibility only follows if there is dependency in existence, and the dependency shown here is in existence. This indeed contradicts essential necessity (al-wujūb al-dhātī), which is the source of absolute self-sufficiency. So denying the implication is an error, especially if analogized with God's need (as Creator) for something from which He creates. That analogy is false. Regarding the second (wujūb al-makān), the commentator notes it assumes place exists, which is not the view of the mutakallimūn. Even if place exists, permissibility of void only shows that place does not need the occupant to fill it, not that it is independent in existence. The correct argument for this divine attribute should be: if the Necessary were located in space (mutahayyiz), it would always be accompanied by spatial modes (akwān), which are accidents. Since everything other than God is temporal, and whatever is never free from accidents is itself temporal, God would be temporal—an absurdity.

Key Terms
متمكن
مكان
خلاء
إمكان
وجوب
أكوان
حادث

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that God's need for place is comparable to His need for something to act upon as Creator, so the implication of possibility does not follow.
Correction: The commentator clarifies that the dependency here is in existence, not in action. Need in existence directly contradicts essential necessity, making the implication sound.

Checkpoint
What is the first false conclusion drawn from the proof?
A) The place is impossible
B) The Necessary becomes possible
C) The occupant is eternal
D) The void is impossible

Key Takeaway
The second proof shows that if God were in a place, He would depend on it, violating His essential self-sufficiency; the correct argument uses spatial modes to show such a being would be temporal.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Does your daily life reflect an awareness that God is not confined to any place—that He is equally present in every moment and location? When you pray, do you truly sense that you are standing before the One who transcends space, or do you mentally restrict Him to a direction? Commit to reminding yourself before each prayer: 'Allah is not in a place; He is with me by His knowledge.'
?
The proof shows that dependency on place contradicts God's absolute self-sufficiency. Is there any area where you subconsciously think God needs something from you—your worship, your obedience—as if He were dependent on your actions? Repent from such a thought and affirm that He is the Self-Sufficient, and you are the one in need.
?
If God were in a place, He would be subject to change and temporality. How does this truth impact your trust in His eternal, unchanging promises? Can you identify a fear or anxiety that stems from forgetting that God is not bound by time or space? Write down one specific worry and counter it with the truth that the Eternal, Placeless God is in control.

Third Proof: God Cannot Be in Any Space
Presents the third proof that God cannot be in any space, with objections and replies.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the third proof from the mutakallimūn that God cannot be in any place, and how objections are refuted.

Core Idea
If God were in space, He would either be in some or all spaces; both lead to absurdity.

Sanusi presents the third proof that God is not in any place. The argument is a dilemma: either God is in some spaces or all spaces. Each option is shown to be rationally impossible. This lesson walks through the reasoning and the refutation of objections.

Guided Path
1
Read the main dilemma
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to grasp the two alternatives: God in some spaces or all spaces.
Notice that the proof starts with a logical dichotomy.
2
Analyze the first alternative
Understand why being in some spaces is impossible: spaces are equal, so specification would be arbitrary unless an external factor determines it, which would imply need.
The concept of tarjīḥ bilā murajjiḥ is key.
3
Analyze the second alternative
See why being in all spaces leads to interpenetration with bodies and mixing with impurities, both of which are impossible for God.
The commentator also refutes al-Abharī's objection about genus.
4
Examine objections and replies
Read the objections regarding God's special relation to spaces and infinite regress of places, and how they are refuted.
The reply about motion and temporality is particularly important.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثالث لو كان في مكان فإما أن يكون في بعض الأحياز أو في جميعها وكلاهما باطل. أما الأول فلتساوي الأحياز في أنفسها لأن المكان عند المتكلمين هو الخلاء المتشابه وتساوي نسبته إليها وحينئذ فيكون اختصاصه ببعضها دون بعض آخر منها ترجيحا بلا مرجح إن لم يكن هناك مخصص من خارج أو يلزم الاحتياج. وأما الثاني فلأنه يلزم تداخل المتحيزين لأن بعض الأحياز مشغول بالأجسام وإنه أي تداخل المتحيزين مطلقاً محال بالضروة وأيضاً فيلزم مخالطته لقاذورات العالم تعالى عن ذلك علواً كبيراً.
قوله فيكون اختصاصه ببعضها إلخ) قيل لم لا يجوز أن يكون لذاته تعالى نسبة مخصوصة إلى بعض الأحياز أو يكون المخصص هو الإرادة، وأجيب عن الأول بأنه لا مجال لاختلاف النسبة فيما يشابه المنسوب إليه، وعن الثاني بأن استناد التمكن إلى الإادة يوجب حدوثه، والمتمكن يستحيل وجوده بدون المكان كما سبق، والخصم أيضاً معترف به فإن قلت لم لا يجوز أن يكون قبل هذا المكان في مكان آخر إلى نهاية فلا يلزم الانفكاك عن مطلق المكان قلت : الانتقال من مكان إلى مكان لا يكون إلا بالحركة وهذا معلوم ضرورة والحركة حادثة قطعاً، فيلزم كونه تعالي محلاً للحوادث فيلزم حدوثه تعالى عن ذلك علواً كبيراً. قوله : (أي تداخل المتحيزين مطلقاً) أشار بقوله مطلقاً إلى رد ما ذكره الأبهري من أن استحالة تداخل المتحيزين فيما إذا كانا من جنس واحد وهاهنا ليس كذلك.
The third: If He were in a place, then either He is in some spaces (ahyāz) or in all of them, and both are false. As for the first, because spaces are equal in themselves, since place according to the mutakallimūn is the similar void, and His relation to them is equal; and then His being specified to some of them to the exclusion of others would be a preference without a preferrer, unless there is an external particularizer, or need would follow. As for the second, because the interpenetration of two located things (tadākhul al-mutahayyizayn) would follow, since some spaces are occupied by bodies, and interpenetration absolutely is impossible by necessity, and also His mixing with the impurities of the world would follow — exalted is He above that, a great exaltation. His statement: 'then His being specified to some of them etc.' — it was said: Why is it not permissible that His essence, exalted, has a specific relation to some spaces, or that the particularizer is the will? And it was answered regarding the first: there is no room for difference in relation regarding what is similar to the related; and regarding the second: because the dependence of occupancy (tamakkun) on the will would necessitate its temporality, and the occupant is impossible to exist without its place as before, and the adversary also admits this. For if you say: Why is it not permissible that before this place He was in another place ad infinitum, so that separation from absolute place does not follow? I say: Transfer from place to place occurs only through motion, and this is known necessarily, and motion is certainly temporal; so His being a locus for accidents would follow, and then His temporality would follow — exalted is He above that, a great exaltation. His statement: 'i.e., the interpenetration of two located things absolutely' — he indicated by his saying 'absolutely' to refute what al-Abharī mentioned: that the impossibility of interpenetration of two located things is only when they are of one genus, and here such is not the case.
Source pages p. 23

Commentary
This passage is the third proof (al-thālith). It presents a dilemma: If God were in a place, either He is in some spaces (ahyāz) or in all spaces. Both alternatives are false. The first alternative is refuted because spaces are intrinsically equal; since place for the mutakallimūn is the similar void (al-khalā’ al-mutashābih), God's relation to all spaces is equal. Choosing some over others would be arbitrary (tarjīḥ bilā murajjiḥ) unless there is an external particularizer (mukhassis). If there is an external particularizer, then God would be in need of that factor (iḥtiyāj). The second alternative is refuted because it would entail interpenetration (tadākhul) of God and bodies occupying spaces, which is absolutely impossible. Also, it would lead to God mixing with impurities. The commentator then addresses objections. One objection asks: Could God's essence have a special relation to some spaces, or could His will be the particularizer? The answer: Concerning the first, difference in relation is impossible when the related is similar; concerning the second, if occupancy (tamakkun) depends on will, then occupancy would be temporal, but the occupant cannot exist without place. Another objection asks about infinite regress of places: Could God move from place to place? The answer: Motion is temporal, so if God moves, He becomes a locus for accidents, leading to temporality (ḥudūth). Finally, the commentator refutes al-Abharī's claim that interpenetration is only impossible when the two bodies are of the same genus; the author insists it is absolutely impossible.

Key Terms
أحياز
خلاء متشابه
ترجيح بلا مرجح
مخصص
تداخل
حركة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that God could occupy a space by His will without needing a particularizer, making His presence in a specific space acceptable.
Correction: The reply shows that if occupancy depends on will, then occupancy would be temporal, and God cannot be temporal. He cannot be subject to temporal attributes.

Checkpoint
Why is it impossible for God to be in all spaces?
A) Because spaces are equal
B) Because it would lead to interpenetration with bodies and mixing with impurities
C) Because God cannot be in any space
D) Because spaces are infinite

Key Takeaway
God's absolute transcendence of space is proven by the impossibility of both partial and total spatial occupancy.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have learned that God is not confined to any space, not even the heavens. Does your heart truly believe that He is as close to you as your own jugular vein, or do you still think of Him as 'up there'? What would have to change in your daily awareness for this truth to take root?
?
The argument shows that God cannot move, because motion implies temporality. Yet you often ask God to 'come to your aid' or 'descend' in mercy. How can you reconcile your language of faith with the reality that He is unchanging? Is there an area of your life where you are waiting for God to 'move' in a way that contradicts His eternal nature? Examine that and realign your trust.
?
If God cannot be in a place of impurity, what does that mean for your own spiritual state? Do you carry impurities in your heart that you think are hidden from Him? How does the absolute purity of God demand that you purify your life, not just your body?

The Fourth Proof: God Cannot Be a Substance
Sanusi presents the fourth proof and clarifies key terms.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why if God were located in space, He would have to be a substance, leading to two impossible consequences: indivisible atom or divisible body.

Core Idea
If God were located, He would be either an indivisible atom or a divisible body — both impossible for the Creator.

Sanusi presents the fourth kalam proof for God's transcendence. The argument shows that locating God in space forces a choice between two absurdities: the contemptible indivisible atom or the composite, temporal body. This lesson unpackages the proof and the key terms substance, accident, and location.

Guided Path
1
Read the proof's structure
The proof is a conditional: if located then substance, then either indivisible or divisible, both false. Identify each step.
2
Understand 'substance' and 'accident'
The commentator defines substance as the located self-subsistent entity and accident as that which subsists in another. Grasp why location implies substance.
3
See why indivisible part is contemptible
The indivisible part (atom) is the lowliest of existents, unworthy of God. Reflect on how this refines our concept of divine greatness.
The term 'juz' la yatajazza' is central.
4
Connect to earlier lessons
The second absurdity (divisible body) recalls earlier proofs that composition and temporality negate essential necessity. Note the consistency.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الرابع لو كان متحيزاً لكان جوهراً لاستحالة كون الواجب تعالى عرضاً وإذا كان جوهراً فأما أن لا ينقسم أصلا أو ينقسم وكلاهما باطل. أما الأول فلأنه يكون جزءاً لا يتجزأ وهو أحقر الأشياء تعالى الله عن ذلك. وأما الثاني فلأنه يكون جسماً وكل جسم مركب وقد مر أنه أي التركيب الخارجي ينافي الوجوب الذاتي، وأيضاً قد بينا أن كل جسم محدث فيلزم حدوث الواجب . وربما يقال في إبطال الثاني لو
قوله : ( لكان جوهر الاستحالة كون الواجب تعالى (عرضا المراد بالجوهر هاهنا هو المتحيز القائم بنفسه وبالعرض هو المتحيز القائم بغيره ومن البين أنه لا واسطة بينهما بعد تحقق التحيز وأنه يكفي في استلزام كون شيء أحقر الأشياء كونه متحيزاً قائماً بنفسه غير قابل للقسمة أصلاً غاية ما في الباب على تقدير أخذ الإمكان في مفهوم الجزء الغير المتجزئ أن يكون إطلاق الجزء بالمعنى المتعارف عليه مسامحة .
قوله : ( فلأنه حينئذ يكون جزءاً لا يتجزأ) فإن قلت يجوز أن يكون يقبل الانقسام فرضاً لا خارجاً فلا يلزم كونه جوهراً فرداً ولا أن يكون جسماً ، قلت : إن طابق الفرض الواقع بأن يكون فيه شيء غير في الواقع يلزم أن لا يكون جزءاً لا يتجزأ، بل جسماً مركباً من شيئين أو أكثر، لانتفاء الواسطة على الفرض وإن لم يطابق فلا عبرة فلا ينافي كونه جزءاً لا يتجزئ وأحقر الأشياء كما حققناه في موقف الاعراض.
The fourth: If He were located in space (mutahayyiz), He would be a substance (jawhar), because it is impossible for the Necessary, exalted, to be an accident (ʿaraḍ). And if He is a substance, then either He is not divisible at all, or He is divisible, and both are false. As for the first, because He would be an indivisible part (juzʾ lā yatajazzaʾ), and that is the most contemptible of things—exalted is God above that. As for the second, because He would be a body (jism), and every body is composite, and it has already passed that external composition negates essential necessity, and also we have explained that every body is temporal, so the temporality of the Necessary would follow. And it may be said in refuting the second: if... (text cut off). His statement: 'He would be a substance, because it is impossible for the Necessary, exalted, to be an accident'—what is meant by substance here is the located thing subsisting by itself, and by accident is the located thing subsisting by another. And it is obvious that there is no intermediary between them after the realization of location, and that it suffices for something to be the most contemptible of things that it be located, subsisting by itself, not divisible at all. The utmost in the matter, on the assumption of taking possibility (imkān) in the concept of the indivisible part, is that the application of 'part' in the conventional sense is a leniency. His statement: 'because then it would be an indivisible part'—for if you say: It is permissible that it accept division hypothetically, not externally, so that it is not necessarily an indivisible atom nor a body. I say: If the hypothesis matches reality, in that there is something else in reality, then it would be not an indivisible part but a body composed of two or more things, because there is no intermediary on the hypothesis; and if it does not match, then it is disregarded, and it does not negate its being an indivisible part and the most contemptible of things, as we have verified in the station of accidents (mawqif al-aʿrāḍ).
Source pages p. 24

Commentary
This passage is the fourth proof (al-rābiʿ). It argues: If God were located in space (mutahayyiz), He would have to be a substance (jawhar), because an accident (ʿaraḍ) cannot be the Necessary. Being a substance, He would be either indivisible (juzʾ lā yatajazzaʾ) or divisible. Both are false. The indivisible part is the most contemptible thing, which is beneath God. Divisibility would make Him a body, implying composition—already shown to negate essential necessity—and also implying temporality, since all bodies are temporal. The commentator then clarifies terminology: substance is the located self-subsistent entity; accident is the located entity subsisting in another. There is no middle ground once location is established. Even if one takes possibility (imkān) in the definition of the indivisible part, calling it a 'part' is just a loose expression. Then a hypothetical objection is raised: Could something accept division only in theory, not in reality, thereby avoiding being an indivisible atom or a body? The reply: If the hypothesis matches reality, then there is actual composition; if not, it is irrelevant and does not change the fact that it is indivisible and contemptible, as proven earlier.

Key Terms
متحيز
جوهر
عرض
جزء لا يتجزأ
جسم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the fourth proof, if God were a substance, what two possibilities arise?
A) He would be eternal or temporal
B) He would be indivisible or divisible
C) He would be body or spirit
D) He would be created or uncreated

Key Takeaway
The fourth proof shows that locating God leads to either an indivisible atom or a divisible body, both impossible, thereby affirming His transcendence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you imagine God, do you unconsciously assign Him a location or form? How does this proof expose even the subtlest anthropomorphism in your heart? What will you do to purify your tawhid?
?
The proof calls the indivisible atom 'the most contemptible of things.' Yet we often obsess over our own physical existence and status. How does this reorient your sense of self-worth in relation to your Creator?
?
You have just learned that God cannot be a substance—something located and limited. But does your daily life reflect this belief? In what ways do you act as if God is confined to a mosque, a prayer rug, or a particular time? Repent and resolve to worship the Transcendent One without boundaries.

Objection: Could an Indivisible Part Be Merely Hypothetical?
Sanusi presents and refutes an objection to the indivisible part argument.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection that an indivisible part might accept division only hypothetically, and why this objection fails.

Core Idea
A hypothetical division that doesn't match reality is irrelevant; it does not change the thing's contemptible nature.

The fourth proof's first absurdity (indivisible atom) invites a sophisticated objection: perhaps something can be indivisible in reality but hypothetically divisible. The commentator answers by distinguishing between matching and non-matching hypotheses. This lesson examines that dialectic and its resolution.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objection
The objector says: maybe the indivisible part accepts division only in the mind (فرضاً), not externally (خارجاً). Understand the hypothetical scenario.
2
Examine the response condition
If the hypothesis matches reality (i.e., there is actual division in reality), then the thing is not indivisible but composite. If it doesn't match, it's disregarded.
3
Apply to the proof
Since a hypothetical division that doesn't correspond to reality is irrelevant, the thing remains an indivisible part and thus contemptible. This solidifies the proof.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الرابع لو كان متحيزاً لكان جوهراً لاستحالة كون الواجب تعالى عرضاً وإذا كان جوهراً فأما أن لا ينقسم أصلا أو ينقسم وكلاهما باطل. أما الأول فلأنه يكون جزءاً لا يتجزأ وهو أحقر الأشياء تعالى الله عن ذلك. وأما الثاني فلأنه يكون جسماً وكل جسم مركب وقد مر أنه أي التركيب الخارجي ينافي الوجوب الذاتي، وأيضاً قد بينا أن كل جسم محدث فيلزم حدوث الواجب . وربما يقال في إبطال الثاني لو
قوله : ( لكان جوهر الاستحالة كون الواجب تعالى (عرضا المراد بالجوهر هاهنا هو المتحيز القائم بنفسه وبالعرض هو المتحيز القائم بغيره ومن البين أنه لا واسطة بينهما بعد تحقق التحيز وأنه يكفي في استلزام كون شيء أحقر الأشياء كونه متحيزاً قائماً بنفسه غير قابل للقسمة أصلاً غاية ما في الباب على تقدير أخذ الإمكان في مفهوم الجزء الغير المتجزئ أن يكون إطلاق الجزء بالمعنى المتعارف عليه مسامحة .
قوله : ( فلأنه حينئذ يكون جزءاً لا يتجزأ) فإن قلت يجوز أن يكون يقبل الانقسام فرضاً لا خارجاً فلا يلزم كونه جوهراً فرداً ولا أن يكون جسماً ، قلت : إن طابق الفرض الواقع بأن يكون فيه شيء غير في الواقع يلزم أن لا يكون جزءاً لا يتجزأ، بل جسماً مركباً من شيئين أو أكثر، لانتفاء الواسطة على الفرض وإن لم يطابق فلا عبرة فلا ينافي كونه جزءاً لا يتجزئ وأحقر الأشياء كما حققناه في موقف الاعراض.
The fourth: If He were located in space (mutahayyiz), He would be a substance (jawhar), because it is impossible for the Necessary, exalted, to be an accident (ʿaraḍ). And if He is a substance, then either He is not divisible at all, or He is divisible, and both are false. As for the first, because He would be an indivisible part (juzʾ lā yatajazzaʾ), and that is the most contemptible of things—exalted is God above that. As for the second, because He would be a body (jism), and every body is composite, and it has already passed that external composition negates essential necessity, and also we have explained that every body is temporal, so the temporality of the Necessary would follow. And it may be said in refuting the second: if... (text cut off). His statement: 'He would be a substance, because it is impossible for the Necessary, exalted, to be an accident'—what is meant by substance here is the located thing subsisting by itself, and by accident is the located thing subsisting by another. And it is obvious that there is no intermediary between them after the realization of location, and that it suffices for something to be the most contemptible of things that it be located, subsisting by itself, not divisible at all. The utmost in the matter, on the assumption of taking possibility (imkān) in the concept of the indivisible part, is that the application of 'part' in the conventional sense is a leniency. His statement: 'because then it would be an indivisible part'—for if you say: It is permissible that it accept division hypothetically, not externally, so that it is not necessarily an indivisible atom nor a body. I say: If the hypothesis matches reality, in that there is something else in reality, then it would be not an indivisible part but a body composed of two or more things, because there is no intermediary on the hypothesis; and if it does not match, then it is disregarded, and it does not negate its being an indivisible part and the most contemptible of things, as we have verified in the station of accidents (mawqif al-aʿrāḍ).
Source pages p. 24

Commentary
This passage is the fourth proof (al-rābiʿ). It argues: If God were located in space (mutahayyiz), He would have to be a substance (jawhar), because an accident (ʿaraḍ) cannot be the Necessary. Being a substance, He would be either indivisible (juzʾ lā yatajazzaʾ) or divisible. Both are false. The indivisible part is the most contemptible thing, which is beneath God. Divisibility would make Him a body, implying composition—already shown to negate essential necessity—and also implying temporality, since all bodies are temporal. The commentator then clarifies terminology: substance is the located self-subsistent entity; accident is the located entity subsisting in another. There is no middle ground once location is established. Even if one takes possibility (imkān) in the definition of the indivisible part, calling it a 'part' is just a loose expression. Then a hypothetical objection is raised: Could something accept division only in theory, not in reality, thereby avoiding being an indivisible atom or a body? The reply: If the hypothesis matches reality, then there is actual composition; if not, it is irrelevant and does not change the fact that it is indivisible and contemptible, as proven earlier.

Key Terms
جزء لا يتجزأ
فرض
خارج

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that if something is hypothetically divisible, it cannot be called an indivisible atom.
Correction: The objection fails because a hypothetical division that doesn't match reality does not alter the entity's actual indivisible status.

Checkpoint
According to the response, if the hypothetical division matches reality, what is the entity?
A) An indivisible atom
B) A composite body
C) An accident
D) A non-existent

Key Takeaway
Hypothetical division that does not match reality does not negate the actual indivisibility and contemptibility of the indivisible part.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you encounter a doubt about your faith, do you treat it as a hypothetical that you can dismiss without investigation, or do you honestly test it against reality? This lesson demands that only real correspondences matter. Think of one doubt you have been avoiding: will you now examine whether it has real ground or is merely a mental exercise? Decide today to confront it with the evidence.
?
The indivisible part is contemptible not because of its size but because of its complete dependence on the Creator for existence and accidents. In your own life, do you acknowledge that every atom of your being is utterly contingent and contemptible without Allah's sustaining power? How does that realization change the way you view your own worth and your need for Him every moment?
